                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (ToF-SIMS) maps and correlated scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of Na-β″-alumina after cycling in a Na│Na-β″-alumina│Na cell for 110 cycles; a) ToF-SIMS 2D map of Na<sup>+</sup> (Field of view: 100&nbsp;µm × 100&nbsp;µm); Al<sup>+</sup>, Zr<sup>+</sup>, and their overlaid maps are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a–c</a> (Supporting Information). b) Correlative SEM image of the area marked by the rectangle in (a); The mapping and imaging directions are aligned with the applied current direction; c) ToF-SIMS 3D surface rendering of the sputtered volume of 0.9&nbsp;µm × 50&nbsp;µm × 50&nbsp;µm with an overlay of Al<sup>+</sup>, Zr<sup>+</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup>; d. side view of the Na<sup>+</sup> distribution in 3D (c); e) 3D surface rendering oriented with the short axis along the <i>z</i> direction of sputter removal. ToF-SIMS 3D surface rendering of another area was depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). Three types of Na filament: Filament I (isolated micron-sized Na particles) was pointed by the gold arrows; Filament II (isolated line-shaped Na filaments) were pointed by the blue arrows; Filament III (the connection of micron Na particles and line-shaped Na filaments) were pointed by the green arrows.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, similar to the in situ TEM observations, we did not find any correlation between the side phase of ZrO<sub>2</sub> and the location of Na filaments in the cycled specimen as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a,e</a> (Supporting Information). It suggested that ZrO<sub>2</sub> may not contribute to the sodium nucleation and worsen the Na filament growth.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302322-sec-0070-title">2.5 Na Filament Network along Inner Na-β″-Alumina Boundaries</h3>
            
            <p>Based on the experimental in situ biasing results in combination with simulation and the post-mortem analysis, Na filament growth appears to be a critical phenomenon in the Na-β″-alumina electrolyte, which strongly depends on the local microstructure. It can initiate from the interface between Na metal and SE, where GBs are located as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0009">9a</a>. These GBs can either exhibit a high resistance for transport or allow fast ion transport depending on GB structure and orientation. Moreover, the anisotropic ion transport results in GBs inside the SE that are strongly blocking Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport, which can also be an origin for Na nucleation with a sufficient overshoot of the Na chemical potential&nbsp;(<i>μ</i><sub>Na</sub>). Since this blocking behavior can occur for a wide range of GB orientations relative to the external electric field as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0005">5</a>, Na nucleation can take place at these GBs. Apart from GBs, our SIMS analysis further suggests that voids act as sinks for Na and the filling is usually accompanied by filament penetration along GBs. Therefore, a Na-filament network (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0009">9b</a>) can be formed in the Na-β″-alumina during Na cyclic deposition process subsequently leading to a failure of the battery.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302322-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1621164d-4349-4776-8856-c7926dce4390/aenm202302322-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1621164d-4349-4776-8856-c7926dce4390/aenm202302322-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/73d70fc8-52c1-46bd-ae08-484868efc011/aenm202302322-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1621164d-4349-4776-8856-c7926dce4390/aenm202302322-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302322-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302322" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Na filament network formation in the Na-β″-alumina based on its microstructure. a) During Na plating, Na filaments can form starting at the interface between Na-β″-alumina and Na metal anode as well as within Na-β″-alumina, i.e., at some blocking GBs with oblique orientation and at voids where sodium can nucleate. b) Subsequent Na plating processes facilitate the penetration of Na filaments along GBs, leading to the formation of a network that connects the Na metal anode and cathode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302322-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we conducted in situ biasing experiments to investigate Na transport and deposition in a Na-β″-alumina│Au(Pt) multilayer system in a TEM at room temperature. Na deposition occurred not only at the interface between Na-β″-alumina and Au(Pt) but also at selected GBs in the Na-β″-alumina “bulk.” Na filament growth mainly started along GBs connected to the Au(Pt) electrode. From this GB Na filament formation penetrated to neighboring GBs. In addition, Na whiskers grew from the thick frame of TEM lamella. This indicates that Na filament growth can initiate both from GBs at the interface between the SE and the negative electrode as well as inside the SE. Moreover, we could show that Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion is enhanced along some GBs, as Na metal accumulated at triple boundaries between the GB and the Au(Pt) electrode. In addition to GBs, voids can also act Na sink during the Na plating process based on the post-mortem SIMS analysis of cycled Na-β″-alumina, probably due to potential differences at the void surface/GBs. Nevertheless, neither in situ biasing experiments nor post-mortem analysis demonstrated any relationship between the ZrO<sub>2</sub> additive and Na filament growth. Overall, depending on the local microstructure, the anisotropic transport in Na-β″-alumina leads to Na filament network formation along GBs and voids during cyclic Na deposition, which subsequently induce the failure of the cell.</p>
         
         <p>The relationship between the microstructure and Na filament growth as well as Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport was explored by crystal orientation analysis, which revealed randomly oriented crystals in the polycrystalline Na-β″-alumina mostly connected by random HAGBs, whereas hardly any CSL boundaries have been observed. Na filament growth occurred at random HAGBs. Since Na-β″-alumina has a typical layered structure resulting in a 2D ion transport path, the influence of the anisotropic ion transport on the effect of GBs to the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport was investigated based on the combination of the orientation of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion planes and the electric field direction.</p>
         
         <p>In a simplified microstructural model, three groups of GBs were defined based on the orientation relationship of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion planes in adjacent grains: parallel (type I), vertical (type II), and oblique (type III). The blocking behavior for ion migration at GBs depends on the relative orientation of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion conducting planes in adjacent grains and the direction of the external electric field. In the proposed model, two cases result in blockade of ion migration across GBs: 1.&nbsp;In type II b1, the dense Al-O spinel layer in one grain blocks ion transport at the GB from the neighboring grain where the Na<sup>+</sup> ion conduction planes are parallel to the external electric field and oriented towards the GB. 2. In Type III a2, Na<sup>+</sup> ions in two adjacent grains migrate towards their common GB and, if the ion conductivity along the GB is limited, this leads to blockade of Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport. This blockade at GBs seems to facilitate formation of Na filaments and should be avoided to improve the stability of the solid electrolyte. Moreover, simulations of the microscopic Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport in the TEM-lamella demonstrated a significant influence of the anisotropic Na<sup>+</sup> ion migration, resulting in a highly inhomogeneous current density distribution in the specimen. Therefore, the microstructure including GB types and orientation should be taken into account for optimizing oxide based SE performance both in terms of Na filament formation as well as overall ionic conductivity. Highly textured SEs with their Na<sup>+</sup> ion conducting planes oriented along the external electric field would significantly improve the overall ion conductivity and reduce the presence of GBs blocking Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport and thus would be expected to prevent Na filament formation.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302322-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302322-sec-0120-title"> In Situ TEM Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Polycrystalline Na-β″-alumina SE pellets (Ionotec. Ltd, UK, phase content 90–95%) were used in this work. To get access to their GBs in the TEM, a focused ion beam (FIB) (FEI Strata 400S) was applied to obtain TEM lamellas with around 4&nbsp;µm x 5&nbsp;µm large and ≈170&nbsp;nm thick electron transparent area. A ≈100&nbsp;nm thick Au layer was coated before any TEM experiment to the surface of Na-β″-alumina pellet to minimize the charge effect and electric field induced damage during FIB process. Moreover, this Au layer served as the negative electrode together with the Pt layer, which is deposited for FIB process, during the in situ electric biasing TEM measurements. The double aberration corrected Themis Z (ThermoFisher Scientific) and probe corrected Themis 300 TEMs (ThermoFisher Scientific) operated at 300&nbsp;kV were used for TEM analysis. With the usage of scanning tunneling microscopy-transmission electron microscopy (STM-TEM) holder (ZEPtools Technology Company, PicoFemto double-tilt holder), the in situ TEM setup (schematic illustration in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0001">1a</a>) was built to provide the electrical biasing to the lamellae and worked as shown in time series images as Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0001">1b–e</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0003">3b–e</a>. Once the W tip was contacted to the specimen, a positive voltage in the range of 10 – 17&nbsp;V was applied to the TEM Cu grid. Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were driven toward the W tip as well as the Au(Pt) deposition layer side. Meanwhile, high-angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) series images were acquired to record the morphological changes. The low-dose energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) (Super-X EDS detector) elemental mapping was used to explore the composition distribution. Advanced TEM techniques of 4D-STEM and automated crystal orientation map (ACOM) analysis (Nanomegas, OIM) were employed to investigate the microstructure of Na-β″-alumina. The electron beam diameter was nominally 170 pm with a convergence angle of 30 mrad and a screen current of 50 pA to conduct the ADF-STEM imaging, while the microprobe with a convergence angle of 0.5 mrad was carried out for 4D-STEM and ACOM. To eliminate the influence of the electron beam on the material, a beam-blank experiment was conducted as a reference to in situ beam-on TEM experiment. This procedure is explained in detail in the supplementary information.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302322-sec-0130-title"> Modeling the Current Density Distribution of Polycrystalline Na-β″-Alumina</h4>
                  
                  <p>For the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport modeling in the polycrystalline Na-β″-alumina, the actual microstructure of the whole TEM lamella was generated based on the HAADF-STEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0003">3a</a>), the 4D-STEM grain map (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3A</a>, Supporting Information), and the ACOM map (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The 2D surface information is discretized into an array of a single layer of cubic voxels. Each voxel is assigned to a specific grain G<i><sub>i</sub></i> in the microstructure, to ZrO<sub>2</sub> additives in the lamella, or to the electrode. This allows to distinguish different structural situations for the transport modeling, e.g., transport through the bulk of grains, transport across a grain boundary, the insulating behavior of the ZrO<sub>2</sub> additives or the consideration of the charge transfer reaction at the electrode interface.</p>
                  
                  <p>The 2D transport computations are based on an electric network model recently developed by Eckhardt et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302322-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302322-bib-0039_R_d60726710e1598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">39</a>-<a href="#aenm202302322-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302322-bib-0041_R_d60726710e1598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to study the effect of SE microstructure and parent metal|SE interface morphology on the impedance response of the system. The transport description has been simplified by using a resistor network instead of an impedance network to simulate the direct current (DC) distribution in the system. Transport between different voxels is described by local resistors in the electric network, namely 2∙<i>R</i><sub>Bulk</sub> for bulk transport, 2∙<i>R</i><sub>Bulk</sub>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>R</i><sub>GB</sub> for transport across GBs, and <i>R</i><sub>Bulk</sub>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>R</i><sub>Elec</sub> for the charge transfer reaction at the electrode interface. Note that ZrO<sub>2</sub> additives are considered to be ideally blocking, i.e., <i>R</i><sub>ZrO2</sub> = ∞&nbsp;Ω. The magnitude of the local resistances <i>R<sub>i</sub></i> depends on the conductivities <i>σ<sub>i</sub></i> and the structural resolution, i.e., <i>R<sub>i</sub></i>&nbsp; = &nbsp;1/<i>σ<sub>i</sub></i>&nbsp;∙&nbsp;<i>L<sub>i</sub></i>/<i>A</i> where <i>L<sub>i</sub></i> and <i>A</i> are the length of the transport process and the interface area between two voxels, respectively.</p>
                  
                  <p>For the 2D transport computations, we assumed a ratio of the in-plane (ip) ionic conductivity (<i>σ</i><sub><i>ip</i></sub>) to the out-of-plane (op) ionic conductivity (<i>σ</i><sub>op</sub>) of <i>σ</i><sub>ip</sub>/ <i>σ</i><sub>op</sub> = 10<sup>3</sup>. This is due to the high out-of-plane Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion activation energy compared to the diffusion along the Na<sup>+</sup> ion conducting plane.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302322-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302322-bib-0042_R_d60726710e1680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Chem. Phys.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the anisotropic character of Na-β″-alumina was taken into account in the simulations. This means that the individual grain conductivities <i>σ<sub>i</sub></i> in <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>direction of the 2D sample are not necessarily the same and both rely on the orientation analysis of the TEM lamella. To this end, the projection of Na<sup>+</sup> ion planes onto the sample surface was determined for all individual grains as depicted in the ACOM map (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302322-fig-0004">4</a>). This results in different angles <i>θ</i> of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion plane to the <i>x-</i>axis of sample surface (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). As a result, <i>σ<sub>i</sub></i> with <i>i</i>&nbsp; = &nbsp;(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>) can be expressed as a function of <i>σ</i><sub>ip</sub>, <i>σ</i><sub>op</sub>, and <i>θ</i>. The detailed equations are included in the Supporting Information.</p>
                  
                  <p>The thickness of the GB, including the grain core and adjacent space charge region, was estimated to be 1&nbsp;nm, and an isotropic GB conductivity was assumed to be 20 times smaller than the in-plane conductivity, i.e., <i>σ</i><sub>ip</sub>/<i>σ</i><sub>GB</sub> =  20. For the sake of simplicity, ion migration along GBs was only implicitly considered in the simulations. The ionic conductivity of the backbone of the TEM lamella, averaged over several grains with random/unknown orientation, was assumed to be 250 times smaller than the in-plane Na<sup>+</sup> ion conductivity, i.e., <i>σ</i><sub>ip</sub>/ <i>σ</i><sub>backbone</sub> =  250. In addition, the charge transfer reaction at the electrode interface and the transport within the electrode are assumed to be resistance-free. Further details and illustrations can be found in the supplementary information.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302322-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302322-sec-0140-title"> Post-Mortem Time-of-Flight Secondary Ion Mass Spectrometry (ToF-SIMS) Characterization on the Cycled Na-β″-Alumina</h4>
                  
                  <p>The symmetrical Na|Na-β″-alumina|Na cell was assembled by isostatically pressing sodium foils on both sides of the Na-β″-alumina separator (Ionotec Ltd. UK, phase content 90–95%) at 100&nbsp;MPa for 15 min. Galvanostatic cycling of the symmetric cell was performed on a CompreDrive device (rdh, Darmstadt, Germany) in combination with an SP 200 potentiostat (BioLogic Science Instruments). In total, the symmetric cell was cycled 110 times, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a> (Supporting Information). The working electrode (WE) was polarized for 2 min followed by a resting phase of 1 minute until the current direction was reversed followed by the same procedure. Due to the slightly different electrodes areas a current density of 260&nbsp;µA∙cm<sup>-2</sup> was applied to the WE while 240&nbsp;µA∙cm<sup>-2</sup> was applied on the counter electrode.</p>
                  
                  <p>After cycling, the sodium electrodes were removed from the Na-β″-alumina separator using scalpel and the separator was broken to obtain a cross section of the cell. Before SIMS analysis, the cross-section was polished on the WE side with Ar<sup>+</sup> ions at 8&nbsp;kV for 2&nbsp;h under cryogenic conditions (150 &nbsp;K) using a Leica EM TIC 3X device (Leica Microsystems GmbH, Wetzlar, Germany). For the Na-β″-alumina reference specimen, a cross-section of an untreated Na-β″-alumina sample was polished using the same Ar<sup>+</sup> beam conditions as for the cycled Na-β″-alumina separator. A schema of the geometric set up for SIMS analysis is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
                  
                  <p>ToF-SIMS analyses were performed on an M6 hybrid SIMS (IONTOF GmbH, Münster, Germany). All ToF-SIMS measurements were carried out in positive-ion mode. The topography mode of the analyzer was activated to increase the total ion count and the liquid ion metal gun (LMIG) was operated in the delayed extraction mode. Imaging was conducted with 60&nbsp;keV Bi<sub>3</sub><sup>2+</sup> primary ions at a cycle time of 60 µs as well as low-energy electron flood gun for charge compensation. Both samples were analyzed on an area of 100&nbsp;µm × 100&nbsp;µm with 512 × 512&nbsp;pixel and a total primary ion dose of 2.8 × 10<sup>12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. A mass resolution <i>m</i>/Δ<i>m</i> &gt; 1500 @ <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> 23 (Na<sup>+</sup>) is obtained. For depth profiling an area of 50&nbsp;µm × 50&nbsp;µm with 256 × 256&nbsp;pixel was analyzed using same LMIG and analyzer setting as for imaging (total ion dose of primary ions = 2.34 × 10<sup>14</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). Sputtering was performed with a 2&nbsp;kV O<sub>2</sub> species on an area of 300&nbsp;µm ×300&nbsp;µm with a total ion dose of 4.4 × 10<sup>18</sup> cm<sup>−2</sup>. A mass resolution of <i>m</i>/Δ<i>m</i> &gt; 500 @ <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> 23 (Na<sup>+</sup>) is achieved. Depth of the sputter creators was determined via confocal microscopy (S Neox, Sensofar, Terrassa, Spain).</p>
                  
                  <p>Data were analyzed using the software package SurfaceLab 7.2 (IONTOF GmbH, Münster, Germany). Present overlay images were created using RGB function of SurfaceLab with binning 4 pixels and shift correction. For visualized depth profiles 4 layers were bound in <i>z</i>-direction while overlayers were created with RGB function and shift correction.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

C.K. and J.J. supervised the project. Z.D., Y.T., G.M., and C.K. wrote the manuscript. All authors discussed the results and contributed to the preparation and revision of the manuscript.</p>
